<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_53e1ac5f-cb70-4a36-9c6e-b61cae3be80f'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

  <span id="freeText154864279839593951" style="display:none">"What's the point anyway — Of suffering, dying? It teaches us to live, boy. A man who does not struggle does not live, he survives." <span id="freeTextContainer15183972194806185340">"A man" is the third book I read by Oriana Fallaci and I never get tired of her style, her long, beautiful sentences and capable use of words. I can't imagine how difficult has been for her telling the story of the man she had loved, a very sad story, which cannot help making you hate tyrannies and their mechanisms more and more as you read along.   <span id="freeText15183972194806185340" style="display:none">"A man" is the third book I read by Oriana Fallaci and I never get tired of her style, her long, beautiful sentences and capable use of words. I can't imagine how difficult has been for her telling the story of the man she had loved, a very sad story, which cannot help making you hate tyrannies and their mechanisms more and more as you read along. The book is about the life of Alekos Panagulis, from the moment he tried to kill Georgios Papadopoulos, leader of the junta which ruled Greece from 1967 to 1974, until his death (or, better, murder) in 1976. Fallaci neglects nothing. In less than 500 pages everything we need to know is written: from Alekos' capture, his imprisonment, the tortures he suffered (I am so sensible I almost fainted while reading about them), until his release, his encounter with the author, their love story, Alekos' struggle to demonstrate the corruption in what was known as the Greek democracy and the struggle that opened him the way to his own end. <br />This is one of the book I think everyone, at least once in his/her life, should read. </span>

<div id="review_1597370014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1597370014" />
    <a title="Marina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10570843-marina"><img alt="Marina" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1597370014?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marina" href="/user/show/10570843-marina">Marina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788817153775" href="/book/show/1573886.Un_uomo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1597370014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11818221609553940270">Reading Oriana Fallaci highlights the difficulty of separating art from the artist. I have my moral objections to reading Fallaci due to her pure, I-dare-say pathological, uncompromising hatred of Muslims as a group and Islam as a religion. This makes it very difficult for me to read and appreaciate her work separately from these opinions: I cannot help but feel as if what I am reading is informed by the same worldview that informed her hatred.<br/><br/>Nonetheless, I decided to go through with reading th</span>
  <span id="freeText11818221609553940270" style="display:none">Reading Oriana Fallaci highlights the difficulty of separating art from the artist. I have my moral objections to reading Fallaci due to her pure, I-dare-say pathological, uncompromising hatred of Muslims as a group and Islam as a religion. This makes it very difficult for me to read and appreaciate her work separately from these opinions: I cannot help but feel as if what I am reading is informed by the same worldview that informed her hatred.<br /><br />Nonetheless, I decided to go through with reading this book because I do find it important to try and understand figures that have had in one way or another a lot of power in shaping public opinion. Furthermore, the book is actually a fascinating and informative piece of literature.<br /><br />In this book, Fallaci tells the story of the great love of her life, Alexander Panagulis, who attempted to assasinate the head of the Greek junta in 1968 and was subsequently imprisoned. After his release from prison 5 years later, Panagulis meets Fallaci and a whirlwind romance ensues - the passion is again, almost pathological.<br /><br />The first part of the book was exceedingly interesting. Panagulis' story, his capture, his time in prison, makes for a beautiful book all on its own. I would have given the first part of the book 5 stars in all honestly. However, the story later becomes, for lack of a better word, obsessive. The love between Fallaci and Panagulis is destructive, unhealthy, painful. While the book often reads as a letter of closure, where Fallaci does not hide his faults, it also reads as a letter of pardon, where she excuses his faults.. An emancipated woman, a war reporter, someone that was left for dead while covering a war, shows us that it is still possible to completely idolize someone you love because of what they believe in politically, even though what they act out personally may harm you. While this might seem like love, to me this mostly seems like the destruction of the self.<br /><br />In terms of structure: the story becomes too convoluted - it starts as a political drama, and romance is promised. However, too many never-fully-carried-to-term reflections on the political situation of the world, too many love betrayals, too many dissapointments are described. It reads as a cross between a publicist's work (which Fallaci has indeed mostly written) and an attempt at an epic work of fiction. It does not work out too well. Furthermore, it also does not reach its goal - Panagulis asks Fallaci to document his life as a testament of his work to the people of Greece, but instead, she writes this letter of closure/of apology/of love. Understandable, from a woman that has loved and lost, but what the book promises to be.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11818221609553940270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_770699973" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/770699973" />
    <a title="Mandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5859680-mandy"><img alt="Mandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311797952p2/5859680.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/770699973?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mandy" href="/user/show/5859680-mandy">Mandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer770699973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3212608275760120649">Oriana Fallaci (1928-2006) was an acclaimed journalist, author and political interviewer, renowned for her coverage of wars and revolutions and her often controversial interviews with major world leaders.<br/>Alexandros Panagoulis was a maverick and often solitary figure in the Greek resistance against the 1967 Dictatorship in Greece. He was captured, tortured and imprisoned for many years for his unsuccessful attempt on Georgios Papadopoulos. After the restoration of democracy, he was elected to the</span>
  <span id="freeText3212608275760120649" style="display:none">Oriana Fallaci (1928-2006) was an acclaimed journalist, author and political interviewer, renowned for her coverage of wars and revolutions and her often controversial interviews with major world leaders.<br />Alexandros Panagoulis was a maverick and often solitary figure in the Greek resistance against the 1967 Dictatorship in Greece. He was captured, tortured and imprisoned for many years for his unsuccessful attempt on Georgios Papadopoulos. After the restoration of democracy, he was elected to the Greek Parliament as a member of the Centre Union, but was always haunted by his past and desire for revenge. He died in a controversial road accident in 1976. Fallaci maintained that he had been assassinated by the remains of the Greek military Junta, as he was about to release some incriminating papers.<br />This powerful and mesmerising book, which Fallaci called a novel, is a factual account of Panagoulis’s life and the intense relationship that Fallaci herself had with him. Their tumultuous love affair dominated her life until his death. She was obviously totally captivated by this often arbitrary and damaged man, and the drama of their relationship in grippingly chronicled in this unflinching account. The early parts of the book describing Panagoulis’s imprisonment and torture are particularly hard to read, being both graphic and heartrending in their account of his bravery. Fallaci shows her political understanding in relating Panagoulis’s struggles to those of dissidents all over the world, and shows how the tactics of oppressive regimes resemble each other in brutality and cruelty.<br />The book sometimes becomes little more than a political rant, and there are certainly some longeurs towards the end, but overall I found this a totally compelling read and one of interest not only to those interested in Greek history but to anyone interested in the life of a man who manages to hold on to his convictions whatever happens to him and how he can transcend the evil that oppresses him. What Fallaci doesn’t manage to do is make him likeable and it is difficult to comprehend how such a strong woman became so much under his sway. However, this is a book that stays with the reader, and this reissue is much to be welcomed, especially as it will bring these events and characters to a new audience.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3212608275760120649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1408830047" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1408830047" />
    <a title="Irwan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/183639-irwan"><img alt="Irwan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442987463p2/183639.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1408830047?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Irwan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Irwan" href="/user/show/183639-irwan">Irwan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9788205123403" href="/book/show/16095457-en-mann">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183639-irwan?shelf=finished">finished</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/183639-irwan?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1408830047" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13968696674136736755">A book about a life of a revolutionary man told by his extraordinary lover. A romantic picture of a man alone against the world with his visions and dreams that seemingly only his death could fulfil. His extraordinary lover was Orianna Fallaci, who, I think, is such a good writer, their becoming lovers seems to be conveniently conspiratorial. The hero should die young, and the lover would write his story. <br/><br/>The story is told in the second person voice, You, which cleverly enables readers to immers</span>
  <span id="freeText13968696674136736755" style="display:none">A book about a life of a revolutionary man told by his extraordinary lover. A romantic picture of a man alone against the world with his visions and dreams that seemingly only his death could fulfil. His extraordinary lover was Orianna Fallaci, who, I think, is such a good writer, their becoming lovers seems to be conveniently conspiratorial. The hero should die young, and the lover would write his story. <br /><br />The story is told in the second person voice, You, which cleverly enables readers to immerse in their life, to be annoyed by the man's spontaneous, unpredictable behaviours, as well as to adore him through the eyes of the lover.<br /><br />I am glad that a friend kindly introduced me to this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13968696674136736755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_717956048" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/717956048" />
    <a title="Menia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13384170-menia"><img alt="Menia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598634310p2/13384170.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/717956048?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Menia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Menia" href="/user/show/13384170-menia">Menia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer717956048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12664161134102959238">I admire Alekos Panagoulis a lot and this book was a good reading if you want to know what he represented and what he did. Being a greek myself i feel very connected to this man and fallacis' book made me love him a little more. My concerns regard the story she is telling, it is too personal and she is overreacting in many chapters. She describes him a little lunatic and a person without logic, uninterested in others people's feelings that he happily sacrificed in order to achieve his greater ca</span>
  <span id="freeText12664161134102959238" style="display:none">I admire Alekos Panagoulis a lot and this book was a good reading if you want to know what he represented and what he did. Being a greek myself i feel very connected to this man and fallacis' book made me love him a little more. My concerns regard the story she is telling, it is too personal and she is overreacting in many chapters. She describes him a little lunatic and a person without logic, uninterested in others people's feelings that he happily sacrificed in order to achieve his greater cause. From stories told by his friends and relatives he wasn't like that at all. He was a very sensitive and protective man. He was very logical and he always counted the possibilities before act. Of course he was hard but think what he'd been through and what he fought. It's a great book but the reader must remember that he is reading a part fiction book - mainly the story after his release in 1973</span>
  <a data-text-id="12664161134102959238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3yRNXY7sRHSEVwCMjntIuAjtci7+matoSiw61o06tWFJs002pgMb0s2jI8Sc3zqACQGrKP4hs0T1DJfOoBKLbw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('idB9PwKTjaZjyIkVHWb47Xr+sN6ztq662XAspbes0FofR31UKnzSACo8ql0PworVexJp2LMOtpZmUIG9moTuVA==')})
  }

Un romanzo che cambia forma come cambia forma l’amore. Scortica pregiudizi, ci espone alla vertigine, ci libera. Ha la solitudine, l’audacia, la virulenza malinconica di tutti gli amori non perdonati, che inseguono l’illusione di uno splendore possibile. Un romanzo che non somiglia a nessun romanzo, perché una storia d’amore non somiglia a nessun’altra storia d’amore.\nMa la storia di Guido e Costantino è anche un viaggio attraverso i molti modi della letteratura, un caleidoscopio di suggestioni che attraversa l’archeologia e la contemporaneità... una Roma ventriloqua, lacustre, gli echi della mitologia greca, e una Londra turbìna di stravaganze. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32764675-l-arte-di-essere-fragili"><img alt="L&#39;arte di essere fragili: Come Leopardi può salvarti la vita" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1477425806l/32764675._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_32764675'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32764675-l-arte-di-essere-fragili?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">L&#39;arte di essere fragili: Come Leopardi può salvarti la vita<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/5310935.Alessandro_D_Avenia?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Alessandro D\'Avenia<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.66 avg rating &mdash; 2,273 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2016\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2769433585658990887\">\"Esiste un metodo per la felicità duratura? Si può imparare il faticoso mestiere di vivere giorno per giorno in modo da farne addirittura un\'arte della gioia quotidiana?\" Sono domande comuni, ognuno se le sarà poste decine di volte, senza trovare ris<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2769433585658990887\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;Esiste un metodo per la felicità duratura? Si può imparare il faticoso mestiere di vivere giorno per giorno in modo da farne addirittura un\'arte della gioia quotidiana?&quot; Sono domande comuni, ognuno se le sarà poste decine di volte, senza trovare risposte. Eppure la soluzione può raggiungerci, improvvisa, grazie a qualcosa che ci accade, grazie a qualcuno. In queste pagine Alessandro D\'Avenia racconta il suo metodo per la felicità e l\'incontro decisivo che glielo ha rivelato: quello con Giacomo Leopardi.Leopardi è spesso frettolosamente liquidato come pessimista e sfortunato. Fu invece un giovane uomo affamato di vita e di infinito, capace di restare fedele alla propria vocazione poetica e di lottare per affermarla, nonostante l\'indifferenza e perfino la derisione dei contemporanei. Nella sua vita e nei suoi versi, D\'Avenia trova folgorazioni e provocazioni, nostalgia ed energia vitale. E ne trae lo spunto per rispondere ai tanti e cruciali interrogativi che da molti anni si sente rivolgere da ragazzi di ogni parte d\'Italia, tutti alla ricerca di se stessi e di un senso profondo del vivere. Domande che sono poi le stesse dei personaggi leopardiani: Saffo e il pastore errante, Nerina e Silvia, Cristoforo Colombo e l\'Islandese... Domande che non hanno risposte semplici, ma che, come una bussola, se non le tacitiamo possono orientare la nostra esistenza. La sfida è lanciata, e ci riguarda tutti: Leopardi ha trovato nella poesia la sua ragione di vita, e noi? Qual è la passione in grado di farci sentire vivi in ogni fase della nostra esistenza? Quale bellezza vogliamo manifestare nel mondo, per poter dire alla fine: nulla è andato sprecato?In un dialogo intimo e travolgente con il nostro più grande poeta moderno, Alessandro D\'Avenia porta a magnifico compimento l\'esperienza di professore, la passione di lettore e la sensibilità di scrittore per accompagnarci in un viaggio esistenziale sorprendente. Dalle inquietudini dell\'adolescenza – l\'età della speranza e dell\'intensità, nei picchi di entusiasmo come negli abissi di tristezza – passiamo attraverso le prove della maturità – il momento in cui le aspirazioni si scontrano con la realtà –, per approdare alla conquista della fedeltà a noi stessi, accettando debolezze e fragilità e imparando l\'arte della riparazione della vita. Forse, è qui che si nasconde il segreto della felicità.Alessandro D\'Avenia, trentanove anni, dottore di ricerca in Lettere classiche, insegna Lettere al liceo ed è sceneggiatore. Dal suo romanzo d\'esordio, Bianca come il latte, rossa come il sangue (Mondadori 2010), è stato tratto nel 2013 l\'omonimo film. Sempre per Mondadori ha pubblicato Cose che nessuno sa (2011). Con Ciò che inferno non è (2014) ha vinto il premio speciale del presidente al premio Mondello 2015.Le sue opere sono ﻿tradotte in tutto il mondo.Da questo libro l\'autore ha tratto un racconto teatrale che porterà in giro per l\'Italia.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2769433585658990887\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div Un Dostoevskij lirico, ispirato, comincia a riflettere sulle disillusioni dell’esistenza e dell’amore nell’ultima opera pubblicata prima dell’arresto e della deportazione, esperienze che modificheranno in maniera radicale e definitiva la sua concezione dell’uomo e dell’arte. In questa edizione, al celebre racconto viene affiancata la visione “diurna” di Pietroburgo contenuta nei feuilletons che compongono la Cronaca di Pietroburgo, vero e proprio laboratorio per la scrittura dostoevskiana. Lo stretto legame tra pubblicistica e letteratura, che accompagnerà Dostoevskij negli anni della maturità, viene così a manifestarsi fin quasi dal suo esordio. Il racconto Le notti bianche ha ispirato il film omonimo di Luchino Visconti (1957), con Marcello Mastroianni e Maria Schell, e il film Quattro notti di un sognatore di Robert Bresson       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25545.Oriana_Fallaci">About Oriana Fallaci</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/25545.Oriana_Fallaci"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1221231926p3/25545.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/25545.Oriana_Fallaci">Oriana Fallaci
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,070
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5343062413605209300">Oriana Fallaci was born in Florence, Italy. During World War II, she joined the resistance despite her youth, in the democratic armed group "Giustizia e Libertà". Her father Edoardo Fallaci, a cabinet maker in Florence, was a political activist struggling to put an end to the dictatorship of Italian fascist leader Benito Mussolini. It was during this period that Fallaci was first exposed to the at</span>
  <span id="freeText5343062413605209300" style="display:none">Oriana Fallaci was born in Florence, Italy. During World War II, she joined the resistance despite her youth, in the democratic armed group "Giustizia e Libertà". Her father Edoardo Fallaci, a cabinet maker in Florence, was a political activist struggling to put an end to the dictatorship of Italian fascist leader Benito Mussolini. It was during this period that Fallaci was first exposed to the atrocities of war.<br /><br />Fallaci began her journalistic career in her teens, becoming a special correspondent for the Italian paper Il mattino dell'Italia centrale in 1946. Since 1967 she worked as a war correspondent, in Vietnam, for the Indo-Pakistani War, in the Middle East and in South America. For many years, Fallaci was a special correspondent for the political magazine L'Europeo and wrote for a number of leading newspapers and Epoca magazine. During the 1968 Tlatelolco massacre prior to the 1968 Summer Olympics, Fallaci was shot three times, dragged down stairs by her hair, and left for dead by Mexican forces. According to The New Yorker, her former support of the student activists "devolved into a dislike of Mexicans":<br /><br />The demonstrations by immigrants in the United States these past few months "disgust" her, especially when protesters displayed the Mexican flag. "I don't love the Mexicans," Fallaci said, invoking her nasty treatment at the hands of Mexican police in 1968. "If you hold a gun and say, 'Choose who is worse between the Muslims and the Mexicans,' I have a moment of hesitation. Then I choose the Muslims, because they have broken my balls."<br /><br />In the late 1970s, she had an affair with the subject of one of her interviews, Alexandros Panagoulis, who had been a solitary figure in the Greek resistance against the 1967 dictatorship, having been captured, heavily tortured and imprisoned for his (unsuccessful) assassination attempt against dictator and ex-Colonel Georgios Papadopoulos. Panagoulis died in 1976, under controversial circumstances, in a road accident. Fallaci maintained that Panagoulis was assassinated by remnants of the Greek military junta and her book Un Uomo (A Man) was inspired by the life of Panagoulis.<br /><br />During her 1972 interview with Henry Kissinger, Kissinger agreed that the Vietnam War was a "useless war" and compared himself to "the cowboy who leads the wagon train by riding ahead alone on his horse".Kissinger later wrote that it was "the single most disastrous conversation I have ever had with any member of the press."<br /><br />She has written several novels uncomfortably close to raw reality which have been bestsellers in Italy and widely translated. Fallaci, a fully emancipated and successful woman in the man's world of international political and battlefront journalism, has antagonized many feminists by her outright individualism, her championship of motherhood, and her idolization of heroic manhood. In journalism, her critics have felt that she has outraged the conventions of interviewing and reporting. As a novelist, she shatters the invisible diaphragm of literariness, and is accused of betraying, or simply failing literature. <br /><br />Fallaci has twice received the St. Vincent Prize for journalism, as well as the Bancarella Prize (1971) for Nothing, and So Be It; Viareggio Prize (1979), for Un uomo: Romanzo; and Prix Antibes, 1993, for Inshallah. She received a D.Litt. from Columbia College (Chicago). She has lectured at the University of Chicago, Yale University, Harvard University, and Columbia University.[citation needed] Fallaci’s writings have been translated into 21 languages including English, Spanish, French, Dutch, German, Greek, Swedish, Polish, Croatian and Slovenian.<br /><br />Fallaci was a life-long heavy smoker. She died on September 15, 2006 in her native Florence from breast cancer.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5343062413605209300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
